   1 | # EVOLVE-BLOCK-START
   2 | """ODE discovery seed for normalized multi-dataset amyloid aggregation kinetics."""
   3 | 
   4 | from __future__ import annotations
   5 | 
   6 | from typing import Any
   7 | 
   8 | import sympy as sp
   9 | from numpy.typing import NDArray
  10 | 
  11 | from pysr_harness.equation_session import (
  12 |     constant_symbols,
  13 |     evaluate_expression,
  14 |     feature_symbols,
  15 | )
  16 | 
  17 | 
  18 | def evaluate_symbolic_candidate(
  19 |     X_train: NDArray,
  20 |     y_train: NDArray,
  21 |     X_val: NDArray,
  22 |     y_val: NDArray,
  23 | ) -> dict[str, Any]:
  24 |     """
  25 |     Generic bounded aggregation-rate ODE.
  26 | 
  27 |     This seed starts from a broad mechanism class rather than a specific
  28 |     closed-form solution: a concentration-independent source term can create
  29 |     onset from near-zero signal, while an autocatalytic state-dependent term
  30 |     can sharpen growth once aggregates are present.  Monomer and seed features
  31 |     modulate both routes, and a capacity factor keeps the trajectory saturating
  32 |     toward a fitted plateau.
  33 | 
  34 |     Features: x0 = normalized time, x1 = initial monomer m0, x2 = initial
  35 |     seed/aggregate M0, and x3 = current normalized concentration/state.
  36 |     """
  37 |     x = feature_symbols(X_train.shape[1])
  38 |     c = constant_symbols(9)
  39 | 
  40 |     time = x[0]
  41 |     monomer = x[1]
  42 |     seed = x[2]
  43 |     concentration = x[3]
  44 | 
  45 |     source_rate = c[0] ** 2 * monomer ** c[1] + c[2] ** 2 * seed
  46 |     autocatalytic_rate = c[3] ** 2 * monomer ** c[4] * (1 + c[5] ** 2 * seed)
  47 |     baseline_flux = c[6] ** 2
  48 |     plateau = c[7]
  49 |     capacity = plateau - concentration
  50 | 
  51 |     expression = capacity * (baseline_flux + source_rate + autocatalytic_rate * concentration) + c[8] * time * capacity
  52 | 
  53 |     return evaluate_expression(
  54 |         expression,
  55 |         X_train,
  56 |         y_train,
  57 |         X_val,
  58 |         y_val,
  59 |         constants=c,
  60 |         initial_values=[0.3, 0.5, 0.1, 1.0, 0.5, 0.0, 0.01, 1.0, 0.0],
  61 |     )
  62 | 
  63 | 
  64 | def run_discovery(
  65 |     X_train: NDArray,
  66 |     y_train: NDArray,
  67 |     X_val: NDArray,
  68 |     y_val: NDArray,
  69 | ) -> dict[str, Any]:
  70 |     """Entry point used by the evaluator subprocess."""
  71 |     return evaluate_symbolic_candidate(X_train, y_train, X_val, y_val)
  72 | 
  73 | 
  74 | # EVOLVE-BLOCK-END
  75 | 
  76 | 
  77 | def _load_data():
  78 |     """Load the first dataset for local testing."""
  79 |     from evaluator import load_all_datasets
  80 | 
  81 |     datasets = load_all_datasets()
  82 |     name, X_train, X_val, y_train, y_val = datasets[0]
  83 |     print(f"Testing on: {name}")
  84 |     return X_train, X_val, y_train, y_val
  85 |

  87 | if __name__ == "__main__":

 690 | def _half_response_time(x: np.ndarray, y: np.ndarray, level: float = 0.5) -> float | None:
 691 |     """Return interpolated normalized time where a curve crosses a response level."""
 692 |     x = np.asarray(x, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 693 |     y = np.asarray(y, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 694 |     if x.size < 2 or y.size != x.size:
 695 |         return None
 696 |     order = np.argsort(x)
 697 |     x = x[order]
 698 |     y = y[order]
 699 |     for i in range(y.size - 1):
 700 |         y0 = float(y[i])
 701 |         y1 = float(y[i + 1])
 702 |         if y0 == level:
 703 |             return float(x[i])
 704 |         if (y0 - level) * (y1 - level) <= 0.0 and y0 != y1:
 705 |             fraction = (level - y0) / (y1 - y0)
 706 |             return float(x[i] + fraction * (x[i + 1] - x[i]))
 707 |     if y[-1] == level:
 708 |         return float(x[-1])
 709 |     return None
 710 |

 713 |     """Bounded shape mismatch based on curve slopes and half-response timing."""

 715 |     y_true = np.asarray(y_true, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 716 |     y_pred = np.asarray(y_pred, dtype=float).reshape(-1)

 718 |         return 0.0

 720 |         return 1.0
 721 | 
 722 |     curve_ids = _ARRAY_CURVE_IDS.get(id(X))
 723 |     if curve_ids is None or curve_ids.shape[0] != X.shape[0]:
 724 |         curve_ids = np.zeros(X.shape[0], dtype=int)
 725 | 
 726 |     curve_losses: list[float] = []
 727 |     for curve_id in dict.fromkeys(np.asarray(curve_ids, dtype=int)):
 728 |         idx = np.flatnonzero(curve_ids == curve_id)
 729 |         if idx.size < 3:
 730 |             continue
 731 |         order = idx[np.argsort(X[idx, 0])]
 732 |         x = X[order, 0]
 733 |         true_curve = y_true[order]
 734 |         pred_curve = y_pred[order]
 735 |         dx = np.diff(x)
 736 |         valid_dx = np.abs(dx) > 1e-12
 737 |         if not np.any(valid_dx):
 738 |             continue
 739 |         true_slope = np.diff(true_curve)[valid_dx] / dx[valid_dx]
 740 |         pred_slope = np.diff(pred_curve)[valid_dx] / dx[valid_dx]
 741 |         slope_scale = float(np.mean(true_slope**2))
 742 |         slope_raw = float(np.mean((pred_slope - true_slope) ** 2))
 743 |         slope_loss = _bounded_loss(slope_raw / slope_scale) if slope_scale > 0.0 else _bounded_loss(slope_raw)
 744 | 
 745 |         true_half = _half_response_time(x, true_curve)
 746 |         pred_half = _half_response_time(x, pred_curve)
 747 |         if true_half is None and pred_half is None:
 748 |             timing_loss = 0.0
 749 |         elif true_half is None or pred_half is None:
 750 |             timing_loss = 1.0
 751 |         else:
 752 |             timing_loss = min(abs(float(pred_half) - float(true_half)), 1.0)
 753 | 
 754 |         curve_losses.append(0.7 * slope_loss + 0.3 * timing_loss)
 755 | 
 756 |     if not curve_losses:
 757 |         return 0.0
 758 |     return float(np.mean(curve_losses))

   2 | """Symbolic regression seed for normalized multi-dataset amyloid aggregation kinetics."""
   3 | 
   4 | from __future__ import annotations

   6 | from typing import Any
   7 | 
   8 | import sympy as sp
   9 | from numpy.typing import NDArray

  12 |     constant_symbols,
  13 |     evaluate_expression,
  14 |     feature_symbols,
  15 | )
  16 | 
  17 | 
  18 | def evaluate_symbolic_candidate(
  19 |     X_train: NDArray,
  20 |     y_train: NDArray,
  21 |     X_val: NDArray,
  22 |     y_val: NDArray,
  23 | ) -> dict[str, Any]:
  24 |     """
  25 |     Generic two-channel bounded growth template.
  26 | 
  27 |     This seed is intentionally broad rather than tied to one closed-form law:
  28 |     one channel is a lag-capable stretched rise, the other is an immediate
  29 |     seed-sensitive rise.  A smooth seed-dependent weight lets the fitted
  30 |     constants interpolate between unseeded, lag-dominated curves and seeded,
  31 |     fast-onset curves while preserving one shared symbolic structure across
  32 |     all datasets.
  33 | 
  34 |     Features: x0 = normalized elapsed time, x1 = initial monomer m0, and
  35 |     x2 = static seed/aggregate M0.  All sub-curves are bounded in [0, 1) on
  36 |     the observed domain, then an affine output calibration handles per-dataset
  37 |     response normalization.
  38 |     """
  39 |     x = feature_symbols(X_train.shape[1])
  40 |     c = constant_symbols(10)
  41 | 
  42 |     time = x[0]
  43 |     monomer = x[1]
  44 |     seed = x[2]
  45 | 
  46 |     lag_rate = c[0] ** 2 * monomer ** c[1]
  47 |     fast_rate = c[2] ** 2 * monomer ** c[3] * sp.exp(c[4] * seed)
  48 |     stretch = sp.exp(c[5])
  49 |     weight = 1 / (1 + sp.exp(-(c[6] + c[7] * seed)))
  50 |     plateau = c[8]
  51 |     baseline = c[9]
  52 | 
  53 |     lagged_growth = 1 - sp.exp(-lag_rate * time ** stretch)
  54 |     fast_growth = 1 - sp.exp(-fast_rate * time)
  55 |     expression = plateau * ((1 - weight) * lagged_growth + weight * fast_growth) + baseline
  56 | 
  57 |     return evaluate_expression(
  58 |         expression,
  59 |         X_train,
  60 |         y_train,

  70 | _SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE = threading.local()
  71 | 
  72 | 
  73 | @contextmanager
  74 | def single_equation_evaluation():
  75 |     """
  76 |     Enforce the benchmark contract for one evaluate_symbolic_candidate() call.
  77 | 
  78 |     A candidate may build helper subexpressions, but it may submit exactly one
  79 |     symbolic template to the scorer. This prevents reward hacking where a
  80 |     program tries many equations and returns whichever receives the best score.
  81 |     """
  82 |     previous = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
  83 |     state = _SingleEquationState()
  84 |     _SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE.state = state
  85 |     try:
  86 |         yield
  87 |         if state.calls != 1:
  88 |             raise SingleEquationViolation(
  89 |                 "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() must call "
  90 |                 "evaluate_expression() exactly once with one symbolic equation template. "
  91 |                 f"It submitted {state.calls} templates. Build one base equation and let "
  92 |                 "the harness fit that equation's constants per dataset."
  93 |             )
  94 |     finally:
  95 |         _SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE.state = previous
  96 | 
  97 | 
  98 | def _record_scored_template(template: sp.Expr) -> None:
  99 |     state = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
 100 |     if state is None:
 101 |         return
 102 | 
 103 |     state.calls += 1
 104 |     fingerprint = sp.srepr(template)
 105 |     if state.template_fingerprint is None:
 106 |         state.template_fingerprint = fingerprint
 107 |         return
 108 | 
 109 |     if state.template_fingerprint != fingerprint:
 110 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 111 |             "Single-equation violation: one evaluate_symbolic_candidate() call "
 112 |             "submitted multiple different symbolic equation templates. The base "
 113 |             "equation must be fixed; only fitted constants may change."
 114 |         )
 115 | 
 116 |     raise SingleEquationViolation(
 117 |         "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() called "
 118 |         "evaluate_expression()/fit_expression_constants() more than once. Do not "
 119 |         "try multiple equation templates or multiple scorer calls and pick the "

  63 | @dataclass
  64 | class _SingleEquationState:
  65 |     calls: int = 0
  66 |     template_fingerprint: str | None = None
  67 |     result_snapshot: dict[str, Any] | None = None
  68 | 
  69 | 
  70 | _SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE = threading.local()
  71 | 
  72 | 
  73 | @contextmanager
  74 | def single_equation_evaluation():
  75 |     """
  76 |     Enforce the benchmark contract for one evaluate_symbolic_candidate() call.
  77 | 
  78 |     A candidate may build helper subexpressions, but it may submit exactly one
  79 |     symbolic template to the scorer. This prevents reward hacking where a
  80 |     program tries many equations and returns whichever receives the best score.
  81 |     """
  82 |     previous = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
  83 |     state = _SingleEquationState()
  84 |     _SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE.state = state
  85 |     try:
  86 |         yield
  87 |         if state.calls != 1:
  88 |             raise SingleEquationViolation(
  89 |                 "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() must call "
  90 |                 "evaluate_expression() exactly once with one symbolic equation template. "
  91 |                 f"It submitted {state.calls} templates. Build one base equation and let "
  92 |                 "the harness fit that equation's constants per dataset."
  93 |             )
  94 |     finally:
  95 |         _SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE.state = previous
  96 | 
  97 | 
  98 | def _record_scored_template(template: sp.Expr) -> None:
  99 |     state = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
 100 |     if state is None:
 101 |         return
 102 | 
 103 |     state.calls += 1
 104 |     fingerprint = sp.srepr(template)
 105 |     if state.template_fingerprint is None:
 106 |         state.template_fingerprint = fingerprint
 107 |         return
 108 | 
 109 |     if state.template_fingerprint != fingerprint:
 110 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 111 |             "Single-equation violation: one evaluate_symbolic_candidate() call "
 112 |             "submitted multiple different symbolic equation templates. The base "
 113 |             "equation must be fixed; only fitted constants may change."
 114 |         )
 115 | 
 116 |     raise SingleEquationViolation(
 117 |         "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() called "
 118 |         "evaluate_expression()/fit_expression_constants() more than once. Do not "
 119 |         "try multiple equation templates or multiple scorer calls and pick the "
 120 |         "best result; submit exactly one symbolic expression."
 121 |     )
 122 | 
 123 | 
 124 | def _record_scorer_result(result: dict[str, Any]) -> None:
 125 |     state = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
 126 |     if state is not None:
 127 |         state.result_snapshot = copy.deepcopy(result)
 128 | 
 129 | 
 130 | def validate_single_equation_result(result: Any) -> None:
 131 |     """
 132 |     Ensure the candidate returns the actual scorer output, unchanged.
 133 | 
 134 |     This closes the loophole where a program calls evaluate_expression() once
 135 |     to satisfy the call-count guard, then fabricates or edits the metrics it
 136 |     returns to the evaluator.
 137 |     """
 138 |     state = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
 139 |     if state is None:
 140 |         return
 141 | 
 142 |     if state.result_snapshot is None:
 143 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 144 |             "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() must return "
 145 |             "the dictionary produced by evaluate_expression()."
 146 |         )
 147 | 
 148 |     if result != state.result_snapshot:
 149 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 150 |             "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() must return "
 151 |             "the exact, unmodified result from its single evaluate_expression() "
 152 |             "call. Do not fabricate, overwrite, wrap, or post-process metrics."

 712 | def _curve_shape_loss(X: np.ndarray, y_true: np.ndarray, y_pred: np.ndarray) -> float:
 713 |     """Bounded shape mismatch based on curve slopes and half-response timing."""
 714 |     X = np.asarray(X, dtype=float)
 715 |     y_true = np.asarray(y_true, dtype=float).reshape(-1)

 718 |         return 0.0
 719 |     if not np.all(np.isfinite(y_pred)):
 720 |         return 1.0

 723 |     if curve_ids is None or curve_ids.shape[0] != X.shape[0]:
 724 |         curve_ids = np.zeros(X.shape[0], dtype=int)
 725 | 
 726 |     curve_losses: list[float] = []
 727 |     for curve_id in dict.fromkeys(np.asarray(curve_ids, dtype=int)):
 728 |         idx = np.flatnonzero(curve_ids == curve_id)
 729 |         if idx.size < 3:
 730 |             continue
 731 |         order = idx[np.argsort(X[idx, 0])]
 732 |         x = X[order, 0]
 733 |         true_curve = y_true[order]
 734 |         pred_curve = y_pred[order]
 735 |         dx = np.diff(x)
 736 |         valid_dx = np.abs(dx) > 1e-12
 737 |         if not np.any(valid_dx):
 738 |             continue
 739 |         true_slope = np.diff(true_curve)[valid_dx] / dx[valid_dx]
 740 |         pred_slope = np.diff(pred_curve)[valid_dx] / dx[valid_dx]
 741 |         slope_scale = float(np.mean(true_slope**2))
 742 |         slope_raw = float(np.mean((pred_slope - true_slope) ** 2))
 743 |         slope_loss = _bounded_loss(slope_raw / slope_scale) if slope_scale > 0.0 else _bounded_loss(slope_raw)
 744 | 
 745 |         true_half = _half_response_time(x, true_curve)
 746 |         pred_half = _half_response_time(x, pred_curve)
 747 |         if true_half is None and pred_half is None:
 748 |             timing_loss = 0.0
 749 |         elif true_half is None or pred_half is None:
 750 |             timing_loss = 1.0
 751 |         else:
 752 |             timing_loss = min(abs(float(pred_half) - float(true_half)), 1.0)
 753 |

 682 | def _bounded_loss(value: float) -> float:
 683 |     """Map a nonnegative loss to [0, 1), preserving order."""
 684 |     if not np.isfinite(value):
 685 |         return 1.0
 686 |     value = max(float(value), 0.0)
 687 |     return float(value / (1.0 + value))
 688 | 
 689 | 
 690 | def _half_response_time(x: np.ndarray, y: np.ndarray, level: float = 0.5) -> float | None:
 691 |     """Return interpolated normalized time where a curve crosses a response level."""
 692 |     x = np.asarray(x, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 693 |     y = np.asarray(y, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 694 |     if x.size < 2 or y.size != x.size:
 695 |         return None
 696 |     order = np.argsort(x)
 697 |     x = x[order]
 698 |     y = y[order]
 699 |     for i in range(y.size - 1):
 700 |         y0 = float(y[i])
 701 |         y1 = float(y[i + 1])
 702 |         if y0 == level:
 703 |             return float(x[i])
 704 |         if (y0 - level) * (y1 - level) <= 0.0 and y0 != y1:
 705 |             fraction = (level - y0) / (y1 - y0)
 706 |             return float(x[i] + fraction * (x[i + 1] - x[i]))
 707 |     if y[-1] == level:
 708 |         return float(x[-1])
 709 |     return None

Knowles ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0002-7879-0140 2 , 6 Show authors Nature Reviews Physics volume 5 , pages 379\u2013397 ( 2023 ) Cite this article 8590 Accesses 166 Citations 23 Altmetric Metrics details Subjects Biological physics Biophysics Abstract The formation of amyloid fibrils is a general class of protein self-assembly behaviour, which is associated with both functional biology and the development of a number of disorders, such as Alzheimer and Parkinson diseases. In this Review, we discuss how general physical concepts from the study of phase transitions can be used to illuminate the fundamental mechanisms of amyloid self-assembly. We summarize progress in the efforts to describe the essential biophysical features of amyloid self-assembly as a nucleation-and-growth process and discuss how master equation approaches can reveal the key molecular pathways underlying this process, including the role of secondary nucleation. Additionally, we outline how non-classical aspects of aggregate formation involving oligomers or biomolecular condensates have emerged, inspiring developments in understanding, modelling and modulating complex protein assembly pathways. Finally, we consider how these concepts can be applied to kinetics-based drug discovery and therapeutic design to develop treatments for protein aggregation diseases. Key points Amyloid formation is a transition between the dilute solution phase of a protein and a solid \u03b2-sheet-rich aggregated phase that occurs through nucleation-and-growth processes. Uncontrolled formation of amyloid aggregates from normally soluble proteins is a general form of molecular malfunction. Kinetic analysis using master equation approaches reveals the fundamental molecular steps underpinning this transition. Processes such as secondary nucleation and the formation of on-pathway or off-pathway oligomers and liquid condensate intermediate states lead to a complex network of reaction pathways.   73 | @contextmanager
  74 | def single_equation_evaluation():
  75 |     """
  76 |     Enforce the benchmark contract for one evaluate_symbolic_candidate() call.
  77 | 
  78 |     A candidate may build helper subexpressions, but it may submit exactly one
  79 |     symbolic template to the scorer. This prevents reward hacking where a
  80 |     program tries many equations and returns whichever receives the best score.

  92 |                 "the harness fit that equation's constants per dataset."
  93 |             )

  95 |         _SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE.state = previous

 116 |     raise SingleEquationViolation(
 117 |         "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() called "
 118 |         "evaluate_expression()/fit_expression_constants() more than once. Do not "
 119 |         "try multiple equation templates or multiple scorer calls and pick the "
 120 |         "best result; submit exactly one symbolic expression."
 121 |     )
 122 | 
 123 | 
 124 | def _record_scorer_result(result: dict[str, Any]) -> None:
 125 |     state = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
 126 |     if state is not None:
 127 |         state.result_snapshot = copy.deepcopy(result)
 128 | 
 129 | 
 130 | def validate_single_equation_result(result: Any) -> None:
 131 |     """
 132 |     Ensure the candidate returns the actual scorer output, unchanged.
 133 | 
 134 |     This closes the loophole where a program calls evaluate_expression() once
 135 |     to satisfy the call-count guard, then fabricates or edits the metrics it
 136 |     returns to the evaluator.
 137 |     """
 138 |     state = getattr(_SINGLE_EQUATION_STATE, "state", None)
 139 |     if state is None:
 140 |         return
 141 | 
 142 |     if state.result_snapshot is None:
 143 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 144 |             "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() must return "
 145 |             "the dictionary produced by evaluate_expression()."
 146 |         )
 147 | 
 148 |     if result != state.result_snapshot:
 149 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 150 |             "Single-equation violation: evaluate_symbolic_candidate() must return "
 151 |             "the exact, unmodified result from its single evaluate_expression() "
 152 |             "call. Do not fabricate, overwrite, wrap, or post-process metrics."
 153 |         )
 154 | 
 155 | 
 156 | def feature_symbols(n_features: int) -> list[sp.Symbol]:
 157 |     """Create PySR-compatible feature symbols x0, x1, ..."""
 158 |     return create_sympy_symbols([f"x{i}" for i in range(n_features)])
 159 | 
 160 | 
 161 | def constant_symbols(n_constants: int) -> list[sp.Symbol]:
 162 |     """Create tunable constant symbols c0, c1, ..."""
 163 |     return [sp.Symbol(f"c{i}", real=True) for i in range(n_constants)]
 164 | 
 165 | 
 166 | def pysr_operator_names() -> dict[str, list[str]]:
 167 |     """Return the PySR operator vocabulary exposed to evolved equation builders."""
 168 |     return {
 169 |         "unary": GENERAL_UNARY_OPERATORS.copy(),
 170 |         "binary": GENERAL_BINARY_OPERATORS.copy(),
 171 |         "ternary": GENERAL_TERNARY_OPERATORS.copy(),
 172 |     }
 173 | 
 174 | 
 175 | def pysr_operator_namespace() -> dict[str, Any]:
 176 |     """
 177 |     Return PySR-to-SymPy operator callables usable in expression templates.
 178 | 
 179 |     Operators like ``+``, ``-``, ``*``, and ``/`` should be written with normal
 180 |     Python syntax. Named PySR operators such as ``sin``, ``cos``, ``sqrt``,
 181 |     ``log1p``, ``max``, ``min``, ``muladd``, and ``clamp`` are exposed here.
 182 |     """
 183 |     return {
 184 |         **sympy_mappings,
 185 |         "pi": sp.pi,
 186 |         "E": sp.E,
 187 |     }
 188 | 
 189 | 
 190 | def _as_sympy_expr(expression: sp.Expr | str | float | int, feature_names: Sequence[str]) -> sp.Expr:
 191 |     if isinstance(expression, sp.Expr):
 192 |         return expression
 193 |     return pysr2sympy(expression, feature_names_in=list(feature_names))
 194 | 
 195 | 
 196 | def _complexity(expression: sp.Expr) -> float:
 197 |     return float(sum(1 for _ in sp.preorder_traversal(expression)))
 198 | 
 199 | 
 200 | def _validate_expression_template(template: sp.Expr, constants: Sequence[sp.Symbol]) -> None:
 201 |     complexity = int(_complexity(template))
 202 |     if complexity > MAX_EQUATION_COMPLEXITY:
 203 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 204 |             "Single-equation violation: symbolic template is too complex "
 205 |             f"({complexity} nodes > limit {MAX_EQUATION_COMPLEXITY}). Do not hide "
 206 |             "many candidate equations inside one oversized expression."
 207 |         )
 208 | 
 209 |     if len(constants) > MAX_EQUATION_CONSTANTS:
 210 |         raise SingleEquationViolation(
 211 |             "Single-equation violation: too many fitted constants "
 212 |             f"({len(constants)} > limit {MAX_EQUATION_CONSTANTS}). Use one compact "
 213 |             "universal equation, not an over-parameterized surrogate."
 214 |         )
 215 | 
 216 |     for node in sp.preorder_traversal(template):
 217 |         if isinstance(node, _BANNED_EXPR_TYPES) or node.func.__name__ in _BANNED_EXPR_FUNCTIONS:
 218 |             raise SingleEquationViolation(
 219 |                 "Single-equation violation: conditional, piecewise, discontinuous, "
 220 |                 f"or gated symbolic construct '{node.func.__name__}' is not allowed. "
 221 |                 "Use one smooth global equation template."
 222 |             )
 223 | 
 224 | 
 225 | def _predict(expression: sp.Expr, X: NDArray, feature_names: Sequence[str]) -> NDArray:
 226 |     symbols = create_sympy_symbols(feature_names)
 227 |     fn = sympy2numpy(expression, symbols)
 228 |     with np.errstate(over="ignore", divide="ignore", invalid="ignore"):
 229 |         if isinstance(fn, CallableEquation):
 230 |             y_pred = fn(np.asarray(X, dtype=float))
 231 |         else:
 232 |             y_pred = fn(*[X[:, i] for i in range(X.shape[1])])
 233 |     return np.asarray(y_pred, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 234 | 
 235 | 
 236 | def fit_expression_constants(
 237 |     expression: sp.Expr | str | float | int,
 238 |     X_train: NDArray,
 239 |     y_train: NDArray,
 240 |     X_val: NDArray,
 241 |     *,
 242 |     constants: Sequence[sp.Symbol] | None = None,
 243 |     initial_values: Sequence[float] | None = None,
 244 |     max_nfev: int = 300,
 245 | ) -> FittedExpression:
 246 |     """
 247 |     Fit free constants in a SkyDiscover-proposed expression template.
 248 | 
 249 |     The expression structure is supplied by the evolved program. This helper only
 250 |     performs the continuous inner fit and PySR-style export/evaluation.
 251 |     """
 252 |     X_train = np.asarray(X_train, dtype=float)
 253 |     y_train = np.asarray(y_train, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 254 |     X_val = np.asarray(X_val, dtype=float)
 255 |     feature_names = [f"x{i}" for i in range(X_train.shape[1])]
 256 |     template = _as_sympy_expr(expression, feature_names)
 257 |     _record_scored_template(template)
 258 | 
 259 |     if constants is None:
 260 |         feature_set = set(feature_symbols(X_train.shape[1]))
 261 |         constants = sorted(template.free_symbols - feature_set, key=lambda s: s.name)
 262 |     constants = list(constants)
 263 |     _validate_expression_template(template, constants)
 264 | 
 265 |     if not constants:
 266 |         return FittedExpression(
 267 |             expression_template=template,
 268 |             expression=template,
 269 |             constants={},
 270 |             y_pred_train=_predict(template, X_train, feature_names),
 271 |             y_pred_val=_predict(template, X_val, feature_names),
 272 |         )
 273 | 
 274 |     if initial_values is None:
 275 |         x0 = np.ones(len(constants), dtype=float)
 276 |     else:
 277 |         x0 = np.asarray(initial_values, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 278 |         if x0.size != len(constants):
 279 |             raise ValueError("initial_values must match the number of constants")
 280 | 
 281 |     feature_syms = create_sympy_symbols(feature_names)
 282 |     X_cols = [X_train[:, i] for i in range(X_train.shape[1])]
 283 | 
 284 |     # Compile expression once upfront so the residual avoids repeated
 285 |     # SymPy subs() + sympy2numpy() calls (the main bottleneck).
 286 |     try:
 287 |         _compiled = sp.lambdify(feature_syms + constants, template, modules=["numpy"])
 288 |         _test_args = X_cols + [float(v) for v in x0]
 289 |         _test_out = np.asarray(_compiled(*_test_args), dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 290 |         if _test_out.shape != y_train.shape:
 291 |             raise ValueError("shape mismatch")
 292 |         _use_compiled = True
 293 |     except Exception:
 294 |         _use_compiled = False
 295 | 
 296 |     if _use_compiled:
 297 |         def residual(theta: NDArray) -> NDArray:
 298 |             try:
 299 |                 args = X_cols + [float(v) for v in theta]
 300 |                 prediction = np.asarray(_compiled(*args), dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 301 |             except Exception:
 302 |                 return np.full_like(y_train, 1e12, dtype=float)
 303 |             if prediction.shape != y_train.shape or not np.all(np.isfinite(prediction)):
 304 |                 return np.full_like(y_train, 1e12, dtype=float)
 305 |             return prediction - y_train
 306 |     else:
 307 |         def residual(theta: NDArray) -> NDArray:
 308 |             substitutions = {symbol: float(value) for symbol, value in zip(constants, theta)}
 309 |             candidate = template.subs(substitutions)
 310 |             try:
 311 |                 prediction = _predict(candidate, X_train, feature_names)
 312 |             except Exception:
 313 |                 return np.full_like(y_train, 1e12, dtype=float)
 314 |             if prediction.shape != y_train.shape or not np.all(np.isfinite(prediction)):
 315 |                 return np.full_like(y_train, 1e12, dtype=float)
 316 |             return prediction - y_train
 317 | 
 318 |     import warnings
 319 | 
 320 |     with warnings.catch_warnings(), np.errstate(over="ignore", divide="ignore", invalid="ignore"):
 321 |         warnings.simplefilter("ignore", RuntimeWarning)
 322 |         result = least_squares(
 323 |             residual,
 324 |             x0,
 325 |             loss="soft_l1",
 326 |             max_nfev=max_nfev,
 327 |         )
 328 |     fitted_constants = {
 329 |         str(symbol): float(value) for symbol, value in zip(constants, result.x)
 330 |     }
 331 |     substitutions = dict(zip(constants, result.x))
 332 |     fitted_expr = template.subs(substitutions)
 333 | 
 334 |     return FittedExpression(
 335 |         expression_template=template,
 336 |         expression=fitted_expr,
 337 |         constants=fitted_constants,
 338 |         y_pred_train=_predict(fitted_expr, X_train, feature_names),
 339 |         y_pred_val=_predict(fitted_expr, X_val, feature_names),
 340 |     )
 341 | 
 342 | 
 343 | def evaluate_expression(
 344 |     expression: sp.Expr | str | float | int,
 345 |     X_train: NDArray,
 346 |     y_train: NDArray,
 347 |     X_val: NDArray,
 348 |     y_val: NDArray,
 349 |     *,
 350 |     constants: Sequence[sp.Symbol] | None = None,
 351 |     initial_values: Sequence[float] | None = None,
 352 |     max_nfev: int = 300,
 353 | ) -> dict[str, Any]:
 354 |     """Score a proposed equation template after fitting its constants."""
 355 |     y_train = np.asarray(y_train, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 356 |     y_val = np.asarray(y_val, dtype=float).reshape(-1)
 357 |     fitted = fit_expression_constants(
 358 |         expression,
 359 |         X_train,
 360 |         y_train,
 361 |         X_val,
 362 |         constants=constants,
 363 |         initial_values=initial_values,
 364 |         max_nfev=max_nfev,
 365 |     )
 366 |     nmse_train = nmse(y_train, fitted.y_pred_train)
 367 |     nmse_val = nmse(y_val, fitted.y_pred_val)
 368 |     loss = float(np.mean((fitted.y_pred_train - y_train) ** 2))
 369 | 
 370 |     result = {
 371 |         "equation_template": str(fitted.expression_template),
 372 |         "equation": str(fitted.expression),
 373 |         "constants": fitted.constants,
 374 |         "loss": loss,
 375 |         "complexity": _complexity(fitted.expression_template),
 376 |         "nmse_train": nmse_train,
 377 |         "nmse_val": nmse_val,
 378 |         "combined_score": combined_score_from_nmse(nmse_val),
 379 |     }
 380 |     _record_scorer_result(result)
 381 |     return result

The generalized logistic function or curve is an extension of the logistic or sigmoid functions. Originally developed for growth modelling, it allows for more flexible S-shaped curves. The function is sometimes named Richards's curve after F. J. Richards , who proposed the general form for the family of models in 1959. Definition [ edit ] Richards's curve has the following form: Y ( t ) = A + K \u2212 A ( C + Q e \u2212 B t ) 1 / \u03bd {\\displaystyle Y(t)=A+{K-A \\over (C+Qe^{-Bt})^{1/\\nu }}} where Y {\\displaystyle Y} = weight, height, size etc., and t {\\displaystyle t} = time. It has six parameters: A {\\displaystyle A} : the left horizontal asymptote; K {\\displaystyle K} : the right horizontal asymptote when C = 1 {\\displaystyle C=1} . If A = 0 {\\displaystyle A=0} and C = 1 {\\displaystyle C=1} then K {\\displaystyle K} is called the carrying capacity ; B {\\displaystyle B} : the growth rate; \u03bd > 0 {\\displaystyle \\nu >0} : affects near which asymptote maximum growth occurs. Q {\\displaystyle Q} : is related to the value Y ( 0 ) {\\displaystyle Y(0)} C {\\displaystyle C} : typically takes a value of 1. Otherwise, the upper asymptote is A + K \u2212 A C 1 / \u03bd {\\displaystyle A+{K-A \\over C^{\\,1/\\nu }}} The equation can also be written: Y ( t ) = A + K \u2212 A ( C + e \u2212 B ( t \u2212 M ) ) 1 / \u03bd {\\displaystyle Y(t)=A+{K-A \\over (C+e^{-B(t-M)})^{1/\\nu }}} where M {\\displaystyle M} can be thought of as a starting time, at which Y ( M ) = A + K \u2212 A ( C\n...(truncated)\n\n---\n\n### Generalised logistic function \u2014 Grokipedia\nURL: https://grokipedia.com/page/Generalised_logistic_function\n\nGeneralised logistic function \u00e2\u0080\u0094 Grokipedia Search \u00e2\u008c\u0098K Suggest Edit Sign in Sign in Definition and Formulation Mathematical Form Parameter Interpretation Historical Development Origins and Introduction Key Extensions and Publications Differential Equation Equation Statement Derivation and Solution Properties Derivatives and Gradient Asymptotic and Qualitative Behavior Special Cases Logistic Function Gompertz Function Applications Growth and Population Modeling Statistical and Dynamical Systems References Fact-checked by Grok 5 months ago Generalised logistic function Ara Eve Leo Sal 1x The generalised logistic function, also known as Richards' curve, is a flexible sigmoid function that extends the standard logistic function by incorporating an additional shape parameter , enabling it to model a wider range of asymmetric S-shaped growth patterns observed in empirical data. [1] Introduced by F. J. Richards in 1959 as an empirical tool for analyzing plant growth, it addresses limitations of simpler models like the logistic and Gompertz curves by allowing adjustable inflection points and tail behaviors. [2] This function is particularly valuable for describing processes involving initial exponential growth followed by deceleration toward a saturation level, making it applicable across disciplines such as biology , ecology , economics , and epidemiology . [3] Mathematically, the generalised logistic function can be expressed in the form $ W^{1-m} = A^{1-m} (1 + b e^{-kt}) $ for $ m > 1 $, where $ W $ represents the size or quantity at time $ t $, $ A $ is the asymptotic upper limit (carrying capacity), $ m $ is the shape parameter controlling curve asymmetry (with $ m = 2 $ recovering the logistic function), $ k $ is the growth rate constant, and $ b $ is an integration constant determined by initial conditions. [1] An equivalent form often used is $ f(t) = A (1 - B e^{-k t})^m $, where $ B $ adjusts for the initial value and $ m $ influences the position of the inflection point. [3] These parameters provide four degrees of freedom, enhancing fit to data compared to the three-parameter logistic model, while the function's solution derives from integrating a generalized differential equation akin to the von Bertalanffy growth model but without restrictive biological assumptions. [1] The function's versatility has led to its adoption in diverse applications, including modeling animal and plant biomass accumulation in forestry and agriculture , where it outperforms symmetric models for skewed growth trajectories. [3] In population dynamics , it simulates species growth under resource constraints, such as in studies of bird and mammal populations. [3] Economically, it forecasts technology diffusion and market penetration . More recently, extensions have been applied to epidemiological modeling of disease spread, leveraging its ability to capture non-symmetric curves through numerical fitting techniques like maximum likelihood estimation . Sigmoid Growth Model Updated 3 March 2026 Sigmoid Growth Model is a mathematical framework that characterizes growth processes with an initial acceleration, an inflection point, and eventual saturation due to resource limits. Core formulations include the logistic and Gompertz equations, with extensions like Richards and multi-sigmoid versions allowing for complex, multiphase growth analysis. Analytical methods using parameter estimation and goodness-of-fit measures make these models applicable in epidemiology, systems biology, urban studies, and more. A sigmoid growth model is a mathematical framework for describing the evolution of a system whose growth accelerates, then decelerates, eventually saturating to an upper limit\u2014producing an S-shaped (sigmoidal) curve. Such models are fundamental in quantitative life sciences, epidemiology, urbanization, technology adoption, and any domain characterized by self-limited growth. The mathematical and statistical theory of sigmoid growth models encompasses a wide family of differential equations\u2014most prominently the logistic and Gompertz equations, their generalizations, and multiphase (multi-sigmoid) extensions\u2014with diverse parameterizations, dynamical interpretations, and application-specific variants ( Somefun et al., 2020 , Estrada et al., 2021 , Hedayatifar et al., 13 Jan 2025 , Chen, 2016 , Samoletov et al., 2023 , Crescenzo et al., 2016 , Biswas et al., 2015 , Crescenzo et al., 2024 , Crescenzo et al., 2024 , Mockaitis, 8 Jul 2025 , Mills et al., 2024 ). 1. Classical Formulations and Core Properties Fundamental sigmoid growth models are defined by ordinary differential equations (ODEs) in which the per-capita growth rate is a nonlinear, state-dependent function enforcing finite-time acceleration, inflection, and saturation. Michaels ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0001-6931-5041 1 , 2 , 3 na1 , Daoyuan Qian ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0002-8539-3346 2 na1 , An\u0111ela \u0160ari\u0107 ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0002-7854-2139 4 , Michele Vendruscolo ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0002-3616-1610 2 , Sara Linse ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0001-9629-7109 5 & \u2026 Tuomas P. J. Knowles ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0002-7879-0140 2 , 6 Show authors Nature Reviews Physics volume 5 , pages 379\u2013397 ( 2023 ) Cite this article 8590 Accesses 166 Citations 23 Altmetric Metrics details Subjects Biological physics Biophysics Abstract The formation of amyloid fibrils is a general class of protein self-assembly behaviour, which is associated with both functional biology and the development of a number of disorders, such as Alzheimer and Parkinson diseases. In this Review, we discuss how general physical concepts from the study of phase transitions can be used to illuminate the fundamental mechanisms of amyloid self-assembly. We summarize progress in the efforts to describe the essential biophysical features of amyloid self-assembly as a nucleation-and-growth process and discuss how master equation approaches can reveal the key molecular pathways underlying this process, including the role of secondary nucleation. Additionally, we outline how non-classical aspects of aggregate formation involving oligomers or biomolecular condensates have emerged, inspiring developments in understanding, modelling and modulating complex protein assembly pathways. Finally, we consider how these concepts can be applied to kinetics-based drug discovery and therapeutic design to develop treatments for protein aggregation diseases. Key points Amyloid formation is a transition between the dilute solution phase of a protein and a solid \u03b2-sheet-rich aggregated phase that occurs through nucleation-and-growth processes. Uncontrolled formation of amyloid aggregates from normally soluble proteins is a general form of molecular malfunction. Kinetic analysis using master equation approaches reveals the fundamental molecular steps underpinning this transition. Processes such as secondary nucleation and the formation of on-pathway or off-pathway oligomers and liquid condensate intermediate states lead to a complex network of reaction pathways. Vasilenko 1 , 2 , 3 & Dmitry N. Ivankov ORCID: orcid.org/0000-0002-8224-4118 1 180 Accesses Explore all metrics Abstract Many peptides and proteins self-assemble into large fibrillar aggregates, reaching sizes of several micrometers. This process typically involves nucleation, formation of transient oligomeric species ranging from dimers to assemblies comprising hundreds of monomers. The roles of these heterogeneous structures in initiating fibril growth vary significantly, as only some convert into primary nuclei, the smallest spontaneously elongating assemblies. The initial stages of peptide aggregation remain a critical challenge in understanding amyloid fibril formation. Here, we analyze the aggregation lag time as a function of initial peptide concentration, employing linear regression on a double-logarithmic scale to derive the critical nucleus size from the slope. We show that selection of linearizing coordinates depends on kinetic regime, and derive appropriate scaling for exponential propagation driven by random fragmentation or erosion. The model also proves that linear and surface growth models cannot capture the weak concentration dependence of lag times for several peptides, and erosion or random fragmentation has to be considered. Inclusion of capping and fragmentation mechanisms substantially improves the plausibility of the model. Although peptide length has no clear correlation with nucleus size, extended helical regions including \\(\\upalpha \\) - and other helices may promote the formation of larger nuclei, stabilizing preliminary oligomers and prolonging the lag phase. We quantified the contributions of higher-order oligomers as on- and off-pathway species across multiple peptides, identifying a common critical micelle concentration range.   79 | def default_operators() -> OperatorSet:
  80 |     """
  81 |     Unary/binary operator lists (legacy PySR API).
  82 | 
  83 |     Prefer ``general_operators()`` for the full search space; this remains for
  84 |     harnesses that only tune unary/binary lists.
  85 |     """
  86 |     return {
  87 |         "unary_operators": GENERAL_UNARY_OPERATORS.copy(),
  88 |         "binary_operators": GENERAL_BINARY_OPERATORS.copy(),
  89 |     }
  90 | 
  91 | 
  92 | def default_constraints() -> dict[str, int | tuple[int, ...]]:
  93 |     """
  94 |     Default complexity constraints for a broad operator set.
  95 | 
  96 |     Unary operators use integer constraints; multi-arity operators use per-argument
  97 |     tuples (PySR ``_process_constraints`` format). Operators omitted here receive
  98 |     PySR's automatic defaults (-1 for unary, ``(-1,) * arity`` for others).
  99 |     """
 100 |     return {
 101 |         "pow": (-1, 1),
 102 |         "max": (-1, -1),
 103 |         "min": (-1, -1),
 104 |         "muladd": (-1, -1, 1),
 105 |         "clamp": (-1, 1, 1),
 106 |     }
 107 | 
 108 | 
 109 | def operator_config(**overrides: Any) -> dict[str, Any]:
 110 |     """Build an operator + constraints config fragment with optional overrides."""
 111 |     config: dict[str, Any] = {
 112 |         **general_operators(),
 113 |         "constraints": default_constraints(),
 114 |     }
 115 |     config.update(overrides)
 116 |     return config
